<p><b>Kurt Friedrich Gödel</b> (<span class="rt-commentedText nowrap"><span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt" lang="en-fonipa"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/ˈ/: primary stress follows">ˈ</span><span title="/ɡ/: 'g' in 'guy'">ɡ</span><span title="/ɜːr/: 'ur' in 'fur'">ɜːr</span><span title="'d' in 'dye'">d</span><span title="/əl/: 'le' in 'bottle'">əl</span></span>/</a></span></span> <a href="/wiki/Help:Pronunciation_respelling_key" title="Help:Pronunciation respelling key"><i title="English pronunciation respelling"><span style="font-size:90%">GUR</span>-dəl</i></a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2"><a href="#cite_note-2">[2]</a></sup> <small>German: </small><span class="IPA" lang="de-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/Standard_German" title="Help:IPA/Standard German">[kʊʁt ˈɡøːdl̩]</a></span> <span class="nowrap" style="font-size:85%">(<span class="unicode haudio"><span class="fn"><span style="white-space:nowrap;margin-right:.25em;"><a href="/wiki/File:Kurt_g%C3%B6del.ogg" title="About this sound"><img alt="" data-file-height="20" data-file-width="20" decoding="async" height="11" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Loudspeaker.svg/11px-Loudspeaker.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Loudspeaker.svg/17px-Loudspeaker.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Loudspeaker.svg/22px-Loudspeaker.svg.png 2x" width="11"/></a></span><a class="internal" href="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/f/fd/Kurt_g%C3%B6del.ogg" title="Kurt gödel.ogg">listen</a></span></span>)</span>; April 28, 1906 – January 14, 1978) was a  <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Logician" title="Logician">logician</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mathematician" title="Mathematician">mathematician</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Philosopher" title="Philosopher">philosopher</a>. Considered along with <a href="/wiki/Aristotle" title="Aristotle">Aristotle</a> and <a href="/wiki/Gottlob_Frege" title="Gottlob Frege">Gottlob Frege</a> to be one of the most significant logicians in history, Gödel had an immense effect upon scientific and philosophical thinking in the 20th century, a time when others such as <a href="/wiki/Bertrand_Russell" title="Bertrand Russell">Bertrand Russell</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Stanford&amp;Son_3-0"><a href="#cite_note-Stanford&amp;Son-3">[3]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Alfred_North_Whitehead" title="Alfred North Whitehead">Alfred North Whitehead</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Stanford&amp;Son_3-1"><a href="#cite_note-Stanford&amp;Son-3">[3]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/David_Hilbert" title="David Hilbert">David Hilbert</a> were using <a href="/wiki/Logic" title="Logic">logic</a> and <a href="/wiki/Set_theory" title="Set theory">set theory</a> to investigate the <a href="/wiki/Foundations_of_mathematics" title="Foundations of mathematics">foundations of mathematics</a>, building on earlier work by the likes of <a href="/wiki/Richard_Dedekind" title="Richard Dedekind">Richard Dedekind</a>, <a href="/wiki/Georg_Cantor" title="Georg Cantor">Georg Cantor</a> and Frege.
</p><p>Gödel published his <a href="/wiki/G%C3%B6del%27s_incompleteness_theorems" title="Gödel's incompleteness theorems">first incompleteness theorem</a> in 1931 when he was 25 years old, one year after finishing his <a href="/wiki/Doctorate" title="Doctorate">doctorate</a> at the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Vienna" title="University of Vienna">University of Vienna</a>. The first incompleteness theorem states that for any <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Omega-consistency" title="Omega-consistency">ω-consistent</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Recursive_set" title="Recursive set">recursive</a> <a href="/wiki/Axiomatic_system" title="Axiomatic system">axiomatic system</a> powerful enough to describe the arithmetic of the <a href="/wiki/Natural_number" title="Natural number">natural numbers</a> (for example <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Peano_arithmetic" title="Peano arithmetic">Peano arithmetic</a>), there are true propositions about the natural numbers that can be neither proved nor disproved from the axioms.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4"><a href="#cite_note-4">[4]</a></sup> To prove this, Gödel developed a technique now known as <a href="/wiki/G%C3%B6del_numbering" title="Gödel numbering">Gödel numbering</a>, which codes formal expressions as natural numbers. The second incompleteness theorem, which follows from the first, states that the system cannot prove its own consistency.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-5"><a href="#cite_note-5">[5]</a></sup>
</p><p>Gödel also showed that neither the <a href="/wiki/Axiom_of_choice" title="Axiom of choice">axiom of choice</a> nor the <a href="/wiki/Continuum_hypothesis" title="Continuum hypothesis">continuum hypothesis</a> can be disproved from the accepted <a href="/wiki/Zermelo%E2%80%93Fraenkel_set_theory" title="Zermelo–Fraenkel set theory">Zermelo–Fraenkel set theory</a>, assuming that its axioms are consistent. The former result opened the door for mathematicians to assume the axiom of choice in their proofs. He also made important contributions to <a href="/wiki/Proof_theory" title="Proof theory">proof theory</a> by clarifying the connections between <a href="/wiki/Classical_logic" title="Classical logic">classical logic</a>, <a href="/wiki/Intuitionistic_logic" title="Intuitionistic logic">intuitionistic logic</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Modal_logic" title="Modal logic">modal logic</a>.
</p>
<div aria-labelledby="mw-toc-heading" class="toc" id="toc" role="navigation"><input class="toctogglecheckbox" id="toctogglecheckbox" role="button" style="display:none" type="checkbox"/><div class="toctitle" dir="ltr" lang="en"><h2 id="mw-toc-heading">Contents</h2><span class="toctogglespan"><label class="toctogglelabel" for="toctogglecheckbox"></label></span></div>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_life_and_education">Early life and education</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Kurt_G%C3%B6del&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Early life and education">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Childhood">Childhood</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Kurt_G%C3%B6del&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Childhood">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Gödel was born April 28, 1906, in Brünn, <a href="/wiki/Austria-Hungary" title="Austria-Hungary">Austria-Hungary</a> (now <a href="/wiki/Brno" title="Brno">Brno</a>, <a href="/wiki/Czech_Republic" title="Czech Republic">Czech Republic</a>) into the German-speaking family of Rudolf Gödel (1874–1929), the managing director and part owner of a major textile firm, and Marianne Gödel (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/N%C3%A9e" title="Née">née</a> Handschuh, 1879–1966).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6"><a href="#cite_note-6">[6]</a></sup> At the time of his birth the city had a <a href="/wiki/German_language" title="German language">German-speaking</a> majority which included his parents.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7"><a href="#cite_note-7">[7]</a></sup> His father was Catholic and his mother was Protestant and the children were raised Protestant. The ancestors of Kurt Gödel were often active in Brünn's cultural life. For example, his grandfather Joseph Gödel was a famous singer in his time and for some years a member of the <span title="German-language text"><i lang="de">Brünner Männergesangverein</i></span> (Men's Choral Union of Brünn).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8"><a href="#cite_note-8">[8]</a></sup>
</p><p>Gödel automatically became a citizen of <a href="/wiki/Czechoslovakia" title="Czechoslovakia">Czechoslovakia</a> at age 12 when the Austro-Hungarian Empire collapsed following its defeat in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/First_World_War" title="First World War">First World War</a>. According to his classmate <span title="Czech-language text"><span lang="cs" style="font-style: normal;">Klepetař</span></span>, like many residents of the predominantly German <span title="German-language text"><i lang="de"><a href="/wiki/Sudetenland" title="Sudetenland">Sudetenländer</a></i></span>, "Gödel considered himself always Austrian and an exile in Czechoslovakia".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9"><a href="#cite_note-9">[9]</a></sup> In February 1929, he was granted release from his Czechoslovakian citizenship and then, in April, granted Austrian citizenship.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10"><a href="#cite_note-10">[10]</a></sup> When <a href="/wiki/Nazi_Germany" title="Nazi Germany">Germany</a> <a href="/wiki/Anschluss" title="Anschluss">annexed Austria</a> in 1938, Gödel automatically became a German citizen at age 32. In 1948, after <a href="/wiki/World_War_II" title="World War II">World War II</a>, at the age of 42, he became an American citizen.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11"><a href="#cite_note-11">[11]</a></sup>
</p><p>In his family, the young Gödel was nicknamed <span title="German-language text"><i lang="de">Herr Warum</i></span> ("Mr. Why") because of his insatiable curiosity. According to his brother Rudolf, at the age of six or seven, Kurt suffered from <a href="/wiki/Rheumatic_fever" title="Rheumatic fever">rheumatic fever</a>; he completely recovered, but for the rest of his life he remained convinced that his heart had suffered permanent damage. Beginning at age four, Gödel suffered from "frequent episodes of poor health", which would continue for his entire life.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12"><a href="#cite_note-12">[12]</a></sup>
</p><p>Gödel attended the <span title="German-language text"><i lang="de">Evangelische Volksschule</i></span>, a Lutheran school in Brünn from 1912 to 1916, and was enrolled in the <span title="German-language text"><i lang="de">Deutsches Staats-Realgymnasium</i></span> from 1916 to 1924, excelling with honors in all his subjects, particularly in mathematics, languages and religion. Although Gödel had first excelled in languages, he later became more interested in history and mathematics. His interest in mathematics increased when in 1920 his older brother Rudolf (born 1902) left for <a href="/wiki/Vienna" title="Vienna">Vienna</a>, where he attended medical school at the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Vienna" title="University of Vienna">University of Vienna</a>. During his teens, Gödel studied <a href="/wiki/Gabelsberger_shorthand" title="Gabelsberger shorthand">Gabelsberger shorthand</a>, <a href="/wiki/Johann_Wolfgang_von_Goethe" title="Johann Wolfgang von Goethe">Goethe</a>'s <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Theory_of_Colours_(book)" title="Theory of Colours (book)">Theory of Colours</a></i> and criticisms of <a href="/wiki/Isaac_Newton" title="Isaac Newton">Isaac Newton</a>, and the writings of <a href="/wiki/Immanuel_Kant" title="Immanuel Kant">Immanuel Kant</a>.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (February 2022)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Studies_in_Vienna">Studies in Vienna</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Kurt_G%C3%B6del&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Studies in Vienna">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>At the age of 18, Gödel joined his brother at the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Vienna" title="University of Vienna">University of Vienna</a>. By that time, he had already mastered university-level mathematics.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13"><a href="#cite_note-13">[13]</a></sup> Although initially intending to study <a href="/wiki/Theoretical_physics" title="Theoretical physics">theoretical physics</a>, he also attended courses on mathematics and philosophy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14"><a href="#cite_note-14">[14]</a></sup> During this time, he adopted ideas of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mathematical_realism" title="Mathematical realism">mathematical realism</a>. He read <a href="/wiki/Immanuel_Kant" title="Immanuel Kant">Kant</a>'s <span title="German-language text"><i lang="de"><a href="/wiki/Metaphysical_Foundations_of_Natural_Science" title="Metaphysical Foundations of Natural Science">Metaphysische Anfangsgründe der Naturwissenschaft</a></i></span>, and participated in the <a href="/wiki/Vienna_Circle" title="Vienna Circle">Vienna Circle</a> with <a href="/wiki/Moritz_Schlick" title="Moritz Schlick">Moritz Schlick</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hans_Hahn_(mathematician)" title="Hans Hahn (mathematician)">Hans Hahn</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Rudolf_Carnap" title="Rudolf Carnap">Rudolf Carnap</a>. Gödel then studied <a href="/wiki/Number_theory" title="Number theory">number theory</a>, but when he took part in a seminar run by <a href="/wiki/Moritz_Schlick" title="Moritz Schlick">Moritz Schlick</a> which studied <a href="/wiki/Bertrand_Russell" title="Bertrand Russell">Bertrand Russell</a>'s book <i>Introduction to Mathematical Philosophy</i>, he became interested in <a href="/wiki/Mathematical_logic" title="Mathematical logic">mathematical logic</a>. According to Gödel, mathematical logic was "a science prior to all others, which contains the ideas and principles underlying all sciences."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15"><a href="#cite_note-15">[15]</a></sup>
</p><p>Attending a lecture by <a href="/wiki/David_Hilbert" title="David Hilbert">David Hilbert</a> in <a href="/wiki/Bologna" title="Bologna">Bologna</a> on completeness and consistency in mathematical systems may have set Gödel's life course. In 1928, Hilbert and <a href="/wiki/Wilhelm_Ackermann" title="Wilhelm Ackermann">Wilhelm Ackermann</a> published <span title="German-language text"><i lang="de">Grundzüge der theoretischen Logik</i></span> (<i><a href="/wiki/Principles_of_Mathematical_Logic" title="Principles of Mathematical Logic">Principles of Mathematical Logic</a></i>), an introduction to <a href="/wiki/First-order_logic" title="First-order logic">first-order logic</a> in which the problem of completeness was posed: "Are the axioms of a formal system sufficient to derive every statement that is true in all models of the system?"
</p><p>This problem became the topic that Gödel chose for his doctoral work. In 1929, at the age of 23, he completed his doctoral <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dissertation" title="Dissertation">dissertation</a> under Hans Hahn's supervision. In it, he established his eponymous <a href="/wiki/G%C3%B6del%27s_completeness_theorem" title="Gödel's completeness theorem">completeness theorem</a> regarding the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/First-order_predicate_calculus" title="First-order predicate calculus">first-order predicate calculus</a>. He was awarded his doctorate in 1930, and his thesis (accompanied by some additional work) was published by the <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Vienna_Academy_of_Science&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Vienna Academy of Science (page does not exist)">Vienna Academy of Science</a>.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Career">Career</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Kurt_G%C3%B6del&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Career">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Incompleteness_theorems">Incompleteness theorems</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Kurt_G%C3%B6del&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Incompleteness theorems">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r996844942">.mw-parser-output .templatequote{overflow:hidden;margin:1em 0;padding:0 40px}.mw-parser-output .templatequote .templatequotecite{line-height:1.5em;text-align:left;padding-left:1.6em;margin-top:0}</style><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>Kurt Gödel's achievement in modern logic is singular and monumental—indeed it is more than a monument, it is a landmark which will remain visible far in space and time. ... The subject of logic has certainly completely changed its nature and possibilities with Gödel's achievement.</p><div class="templatequotecite">— <cite><a href="/wiki/John_von_Neumann" title="John von Neumann">John von Neumann</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16"><a href="#cite_note-16">[16]</a></sup></cite></div></blockquote>
<p>In 1930 Gödel attended the <a href="/wiki/Second_Conference_on_the_Epistemology_of_the_Exact_Sciences" title="Second Conference on the Epistemology of the Exact Sciences">Second Conference on the Epistemology of the Exact Sciences</a>, held in <a href="/wiki/K%C3%B6nigsberg" title="Königsberg">Königsberg</a>, 5–7 September. Here he delivered his <a href="/wiki/G%C3%B6del%27s_incompleteness_theorems" title="Gödel's incompleteness theorems">incompleteness theorems</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Stadler_17-0"><a href="#cite_note-Stadler-17">[17]</a></sup>
</p><p>Gödel published his incompleteness theorems in <span title="German-language text"><i lang="de">Über formal unentscheidbare Sätze der <span title="Latin-language text"><i lang="la">Principia Mathematica</i></span> und verwandter Systeme</i></span> (called in English "<a href="/wiki/On_Formally_Undecidable_Propositions_of_Principia_Mathematica_and_Related_Systems" title="On Formally Undecidable Propositions of Principia Mathematica and Related Systems">On Formally Undecidable Propositions of <span title="Latin-language text"><i lang="la">Principia Mathematica</i></span> and Related Systems</a>"). In that article, he proved for any <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Recursion_theory" title="Recursion theory">computable</a> <a href="/wiki/Axiomatic_system" title="Axiomatic system">axiomatic system</a> that is powerful enough to describe the arithmetic of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Natural_numbers" title="Natural numbers">natural numbers</a> (e.g., the <a href="/wiki/Peano_axioms" title="Peano axioms">Peano axioms</a> or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/ZFC" title="ZFC">Zermelo–Fraenkel set theory with the axiom of choice</a>), that:
</p>
<ol><li>If a (logical or axiomatic formal) <a href="/wiki/Formal_system" title="Formal system">system</a> is <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Omega-consistency" title="Omega-consistency">omega-consistent</a>, it cannot be <a href="/wiki/Completeness_(logic)" title="Completeness (logic)">syntactically complete</a>.</li>
<li>The consistency of <a href="/wiki/Axiom" title="Axiom">axioms</a> cannot be proved within their own <a href="/wiki/Axiomatic_system" title="Axiomatic system">system</a>.</li></ol>
<p>These theorems ended a half-century of attempts, beginning with the work of <a href="/wiki/Gottlob_Frege" title="Gottlob Frege">Gottlob Frege</a> and culminating in <span title="Latin-language text"><i lang="la"><a href="/wiki/Principia_Mathematica" title="Principia Mathematica">Principia Mathematica</a></i></span> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hilbert%27s_Program" title="Hilbert's Program">Hilbert's Program</a>, to find a non-<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Relative_consistency" title="Relative consistency">relatively</a> consistent axiomatization sufficient for number theory (that was to serve as the foundation for other fields of mathematics).
</p><p>In hindsight, the basic idea at the heart of the incompleteness theorem is rather simple. Gödel essentially constructed a formula that claims that it is unprovable in a given formal system. If it were provable, it would be false.
Thus there will always be at least one true but unprovable statement.
That is, for any <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Computably_enumerable" title="Computably enumerable">computably enumerable</a> set of axioms for arithmetic (that is, a set that can in principle be printed out by an idealized computer with unlimited resources), there is a formula that is true of arithmetic, but which is not provable in that system.
To make this precise, however, Gödel needed to produce a method to encode (as natural numbers) statements, proofs, and the concept of provability; he did this using a process known as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/G%C3%B6del_number" title="Gödel number">Gödel numbering</a>.
</p><p>In his two-page paper <span title="German-language text"><i lang="de">Zum intuitionistischen Aussagenkalkül</i></span> (1932) Gödel refuted the finite-valuedness of <a href="/wiki/Intuitionistic_logic" title="Intuitionistic logic">intuitionistic logic</a>. In the proof, he implicitly used what has later become known as <a href="/wiki/Intermediate_logic" title="Intermediate logic">Gödel–Dummett intermediate logic</a> (or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/T-norm_fuzzy_logic" title="T-norm fuzzy logic">Gödel fuzzy logic</a>).
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Mid-1930s:_further_work_and_U.S._visits">Mid-1930s: further work and U.S. visits</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Kurt_G%C3%B6del&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Mid-1930s: further work and U.S. visits">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Gödel earned his <a href="/wiki/Habilitation" title="Habilitation">habilitation</a> at Vienna in 1932, and in 1933 he became a <span title="German-language text"><i lang="de"><a href="/wiki/Privatdozent" title="Privatdozent">Privatdozent</a></i></span> (unpaid lecturer) there. In 1933 <a href="/wiki/Adolf_Hitler" title="Adolf Hitler">Adolf Hitler</a> came to power in Germany, and over the following years the Nazis rose in influence in Austria, and among Vienna's mathematicians. In June 1936, <a href="/wiki/Moritz_Schlick" title="Moritz Schlick">Moritz Schlick</a>, whose seminar had aroused Gödel's interest in logic, was assassinated by one of his former students, <a href="/wiki/Johann_Nelb%C3%B6ck" title="Johann Nelböck">Johann Nelböck</a>. This triggered "a severe nervous crisis" in Gödel.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Casti2001_18-0"><a href="#cite_note-Casti2001-18">[18]</a></sup> He developed paranoid symptoms, including a fear of being poisoned, and spent several months in a sanitarium for nervous diseases.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19"><a href="#cite_note-19">[19]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 1933, Gödel first traveled to the U.S., where he met <a href="/wiki/Albert_Einstein" title="Albert Einstein">Albert Einstein</a>, who became a good friend.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup> He delivered an address to the annual meeting of the <a href="/wiki/American_Mathematical_Society" title="American Mathematical Society">American Mathematical Society</a>. During this year, Gödel also developed the ideas of computability and <a href="/wiki/Computable_function" title="Computable function">recursive functions</a> to the point where he was able to present a lecture on general recursive functions and the concept of truth. This work was developed in number theory, using <a href="/wiki/G%C3%B6del_numbering" title="Gödel numbering">Gödel numbering</a>.
</p><p>In 1934, Gödel gave a series of lectures at the <a href="/wiki/Institute_for_Advanced_Study" title="Institute for Advanced Study">Institute for Advanced Study</a> (IAS) in <a href="/wiki/Princeton,_New_Jersey" title="Princeton, New Jersey">Princeton, New Jersey</a>, titled <i>On undecidable propositions of formal mathematical systems</i>. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Stephen_Kleene" title="Stephen Kleene">Stephen Kleene</a>, who had just completed his PhD at Princeton, took notes of these lectures that have been subsequently published.
</p><p>Gödel visited the IAS again in the autumn of 1935. The travelling and the hard work had exhausted him and the next year he took a break to recover from a depressive episode. He returned to teaching in 1937. During this time, he worked on the proof of consistency of the <a href="/wiki/Axiom_of_choice" title="Axiom of choice">axiom of choice</a> and of the <a href="/wiki/Continuum_hypothesis" title="Continuum hypothesis">continuum hypothesis</a>; he went on to show that these hypotheses cannot be disproved from the common system of axioms of set theory.
</p><p>He married <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Adele_G%C3%B6del&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Adele Gödel (page does not exist)">Adele Nimbursky</a><span class="noprint" style="font-size:85%; font-style: normal;"> [<a class="extiw" href="https://es.wikipedia.org/wiki/Adele_G%C3%B6del" title="es:Adele Gödel">es </a>; <a class="extiw" href="https://ast.wikipedia.org/wiki/Adele_G%C3%B6del" title="ast:Adele Gödel"> ast</a>]</span> (née Porkert, 1899–1981), whom he had known for over 10 years, on September 20, 1938. Gödel's parents had opposed their relationship because she was a divorced dancer, six years older than he was.
</p><p>Subsequently, he left for another visit to the United States, spending the autumn of 1938 at the IAS and publishing <i>Consistency of the axiom of choice and of the generalized continuum-hypothesis with the axioms of set theory,</i><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[21]</a></sup> a classic of modern mathematics. In that work he introduced the <a href="/wiki/Constructible_universe" title="Constructible universe">constructible universe</a>, a model of <a href="/wiki/Set_theory" title="Set theory">set theory</a> in which the only sets that exist are those that can be constructed from simpler sets. Gödel showed that both the <a href="/wiki/Axiom_of_choice" title="Axiom of choice">axiom of choice</a> (AC) and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Generalized_continuum_hypothesis" title="Generalized continuum hypothesis">generalized continuum hypothesis</a> (GCH) are true in the constructible universe, and therefore must be consistent with the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Zermelo%E2%80%93Fraenkel_axioms" title="Zermelo–Fraenkel axioms">Zermelo–Fraenkel axioms</a> for set theory (ZF). This result has had considerable consequences for working mathematicians, as it means they can assume the axiom of choice when proving the <a href="/wiki/Hahn%E2%80%93Banach_theorem" title="Hahn–Banach theorem">Hahn–Banach theorem</a>. <a href="/wiki/Paul_Cohen" title="Paul Cohen">Paul Cohen</a> later constructed a <a href="/wiki/Structure_(mathematical_logic)" title="Structure (mathematical logic)">model</a> of ZF in which AC and GCH are false; together these proofs mean that AC and GCH are independent of the ZF axioms for set theory.
</p><p>Gödel spent the spring of 1939 at the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Notre_Dame" title="University of Notre Dame">University of Notre Dame</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22"><a href="#cite_note-22">[22]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span id="Princeton.2C_Einstein.2C_U.S._citizenship"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Princeton,_Einstein,_U.S._citizenship">Princeton, Einstein, U.S. citizenship</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Kurt_G%C3%B6del&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Princeton, Einstein, U.S. citizenship">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>After the <a href="/wiki/Anschluss" title="Anschluss">Anschluss</a> on 12 March 1938, Austria had become a part of <a href="/wiki/Nazi_Germany" title="Nazi Germany">Nazi Germany</a>.
Germany abolished the title <span title="German-language text"><i lang="de"><a href="/wiki/Privatdozent" title="Privatdozent">Privatdozent</a></i></span>, so Gödel had to apply for a different position under the new order. His former association with Jewish members of the Vienna Circle, especially with Hahn, weighed against him. The University of Vienna turned his application down.
</p><p>His predicament intensified when the German army found him fit for conscription. World War II started in September 1939.
Before the year was up, Gödel and his wife left Vienna for <a href="/wiki/Princeton,_New_Jersey" title="Princeton, New Jersey">Princeton</a>. To avoid the difficulty of an Atlantic crossing, the Gödels took the <a href="/wiki/Trans-Siberian_Railway" title="Trans-Siberian Railway">Trans-Siberian Railway</a> to the Pacific, sailed from Japan to San Francisco (which they reached on March 4, 1940), then crossed the US by train to Princeton. There Gödel accepted a position at the Institute for Advanced Study (IAS), which he had previously visited during 1933–34.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">[23]</a></sup>
</p><p>Albert Einstein was also living at Princeton during this time. Gödel and Einstein developed a strong friendship, and were known to take long walks together to and from the Institute for Advanced Study. The nature of their conversations was a mystery to the other Institute members. Economist <a href="/wiki/Oskar_Morgenstern" title="Oskar Morgenstern">Oskar Morgenstern</a> recounts that toward the end of his life Einstein confided that his "own work no longer meant much, that he came to the Institute merely ... to have the privilege of walking home with Gödel".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24"><a href="#cite_note-24">[24]</a></sup>
</p><p>Gödel and his wife, Adele, spent the summer of 1942 in <a href="/wiki/Blue_Hill,_Maine" title="Blue Hill, Maine">Blue Hill, Maine</a>, at the Blue Hill Inn at the top of the bay. Gödel was not merely vacationing but had a very productive summer of work. Using <span title="German-language text"><i lang="de">Heft 15</i></span> [volume 15] of Gödel's still-unpublished <span title="German-language text"><i lang="de">Arbeitshefte</i></span> [working notebooks], <a href="/wiki/John_W._Dawson_Jr." title="John W. Dawson Jr.">John W. Dawson Jr.</a> conjectures that Gödel discovered a proof for the independence of the axiom of choice from finite type theory, a weakened form of set theory, while in Blue Hill in 1942. Gödel's close friend <a href="/wiki/Hao_Wang_(academic)" title="Hao Wang (academic)">Hao Wang</a> supports this conjecture, noting that Gödel's Blue Hill notebooks contain his most extensive treatment of the problem.
</p><p>On December 5, 1947, Einstein and Morgenstern accompanied Gödel to his <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/U.S._citizenship" title="U.S. citizenship">U.S. citizenship</a> exam, where they acted as witnesses. Gödel had confided in them that he had discovered an inconsistency in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/U.S._Constitution" title="U.S. Constitution">U.S. Constitution</a> that could allow the U.S. to become a dictatorship; this has since been dubbed <a href="/wiki/G%C3%B6del%27s_Loophole" title="Gödel's Loophole">Gödel's Loophole</a>. Einstein and Morgenstern were concerned that their friend's unpredictable behavior might jeopardize his application. The judge turned out to be <a href="/wiki/Phillip_Forman" title="Phillip Forman">Phillip Forman</a>, who knew Einstein and had administered the oath at Einstein's own citizenship hearing. Everything went smoothly until Forman happened to ask Gödel if he thought a dictatorship like the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nazi_regime" title="Nazi regime">Nazi regime</a> could happen in the U.S. Gödel then started to explain his discovery to Forman. Forman understood what was going on, cut Gödel off, and moved the hearing on to other questions and a routine conclusion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26"><a href="#cite_note-26">[26]</a></sup>
</p><p>Gödel became a permanent member of the Institute for Advanced Study at Princeton in 1946. Around this time he stopped publishing, though he continued to work. He became a full professor at the Institute in 1953 and an emeritus professor in 1976.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27"><a href="#cite_note-27">[27]</a></sup>
</p><p>During his time at the institute, Gödel's interests turned to philosophy and physics. In 1949, he demonstrated the existence of solutions involving <a href="/wiki/Closed_timelike_curve" title="Closed timelike curve">closed timelike curves</a>, to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Einstein%27s_field_equations" title="Einstein's field equations">Einstein's field equations</a> in <a href="/wiki/General_relativity" title="General relativity">general relativity</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[28]</a></sup> He is said to have given this elaboration to Einstein as a present for his 70th birthday.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29"><a href="#cite_note-29">[29]</a></sup> His "rotating universes" would allow <a href="/wiki/Time_travel" title="Time travel">time travel</a> to the past and caused Einstein to have doubts about his own theory. His solutions are known as the <a href="/wiki/G%C3%B6del_metric" title="Gödel metric">Gödel metric</a> (an exact solution of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Einstein_field_equation" title="Einstein field equation">Einstein field equation</a>).
</p><p>He studied and admired the works of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gottfried_Leibniz" title="Gottfried Leibniz">Gottfried Leibniz</a>, but came to believe that a hostile conspiracy had caused some of Leibniz's works to be suppressed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30"><a href="#cite_note-30">[30]</a></sup> To a lesser extent he studied <a href="/wiki/Immanuel_Kant" title="Immanuel Kant">Immanuel Kant</a> and <a href="/wiki/Edmund_Husserl" title="Edmund Husserl">Edmund Husserl</a>. In the early 1970s, Gödel circulated among his friends an elaboration of Leibniz's version of <a href="/wiki/Anselm_of_Canterbury" title="Anselm of Canterbury">Anselm of Canterbury</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Ontological_argument" title="Ontological argument">ontological proof</a> of God's existence. This is now known as <a href="/wiki/G%C3%B6del%27s_ontological_proof" title="Gödel's ontological proof">Gödel's ontological proof</a>.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Awards_and_honours">Awards and honours</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Kurt_G%C3%B6del&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Awards and honours">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Gödel was awarded (with <a href="/wiki/Julian_Schwinger" title="Julian Schwinger">Julian Schwinger</a>) the first <a href="/wiki/Albert_Einstein_Award" title="Albert Einstein Award">Albert Einstein Award</a> in 1951, and was also awarded the <a href="/wiki/National_Medal_of_Science" title="National Medal of Science">National Medal of Science</a>, in 1974.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31"><a href="#cite_note-31">[31]</a></sup> Gödel was elected a resident member of the <a href="/wiki/American_Philosophical_Society" title="American Philosophical Society">American Philosophical Society</a> in 1961 and a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/List_of_Fellows_of_the_Royal_Society_elected_in_1968" title="List of Fellows of the Royal Society elected in 1968">Foreign Member of the Royal Society (ForMemRS) in 1968</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32"><a href="#cite_note-32">[32]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-frs_1-1"><a href="#cite_note-frs-1">[1]</a></sup> He was a Plenary Speaker of the <a href="/wiki/International_Congress_of_Mathematicians" title="International Congress of Mathematicians">ICM</a> in 1950 in Cambridge, Massachusetts.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/G%C3%B6del_Prize" title="Gödel Prize">Gödel Prize</a>, an annual prize for outstanding papers in the area of theoretical computer science, is named after him.
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Kurt_godel_tomb_2004.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="906" data-file-width="1443" decoding="async" height="126" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fb/Kurt_godel_tomb_2004.jpg/200px-Kurt_godel_tomb_2004.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fb/Kurt_godel_tomb_2004.jpg/300px-Kurt_godel_tomb_2004.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fb/Kurt_godel_tomb_2004.jpg/400px-Kurt_godel_tomb_2004.jpg 2x" width="200"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Kurt_godel_tomb_2004.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Gravestone of Kurt and Adele Gödel in the Princeton, N.J., cemetery</div></div></div>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Later_life_and_death">Later life and death</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Kurt_G%C3%B6del&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Later life and death">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Later in his life, Gödel suffered periods of <a href="/wiki/Mental_disorder" title="Mental disorder">mental instability</a> and illness. Following the assassination of his close friend <a href="/wiki/Moritz_Schlick" title="Moritz Schlick">Moritz Schlick</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pape_Trag_34-0"><a href="#cite_note-pape_Trag-34">[34]</a></sup> Gödel developed an <a href="/wiki/Persecutory_delusion" title="Persecutory delusion">obsessive fear of being poisoned</a>, and would eat only food prepared by his wife Adele. Adele was hospitalized beginning in late 1977, and in her absence Gödel refused to eat;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35"><a href="#cite_note-35">[35]</a></sup> he weighed 29 kilograms (65 lb) when he died of "malnutrition and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Inanition" title="Inanition">inanition</a> caused by personality disturbance" in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Princeton_Hospital" title="Princeton Hospital">Princeton Hospital</a> on January 14, 1978.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup> He was buried in <a href="/wiki/Princeton_Cemetery" title="Princeton Cemetery">Princeton Cemetery</a>. Adele died in 1981.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37"><a href="#cite_note-37">[37]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Religious_views">Religious views</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Kurt_G%C3%B6del&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Religious views">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Gödel believed that God<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38"><a href="#cite_note-38">[38]</a></sup> was personal, and called his philosophy "rationalistic, idealistic, optimistic, and theological".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWang1996[httpsbooksgooglecomaubooksidpckvCy6L_ocCpgPA8_8]_39-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWang1996[httpsbooksgooglecomaubooksidpckvCy6L_ocCpgPA8_8]-39">[39]</a></sup>
</p><p>Gödel believed in an afterlife, saying, "Of course this supposes that there are many relationships which today's science and received wisdom haven't any inkling of. But I am convinced of this [the afterlife], independently of any theology." It is "possible today to perceive, by pure reasoning" that it "is entirely consistent with known facts." "If the world is rationally constructed and has meaning, then there must be such a thing [as an afterlife]."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWang1996104-105_40-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWang1996104-105-40">[40]</a></sup>
</p><p>In an unmailed answer to a questionnaire, Gödel described his religion as "baptized Lutheran (but not member of any religious congregation). My belief is <i><a href="/wiki/Theism" title="Theism">theistic</a></i>, not <a href="/wiki/Pantheism" title="Pantheism">pantheistic</a>, following <a href="/wiki/Gottfried_Wilhelm_Leibniz" title="Gottfried Wilhelm Leibniz">Leibniz</a> rather than <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Spinoza" title="Spinoza">Spinoza</a>."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41"><a href="#cite_note-41">[41]</a></sup> Of religion(s) in general, he said: "Religions are, for the most part, bad—but religion is not".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWang1996316_42-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWang1996316-42">[42]</a></sup> According to his wife Adele, "Gödel, although he did not go to church, was religious and read the Bible in bed every Sunday morning",<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWang199651_43-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWang199651-43">[43]</a></sup> while of <a href="/wiki/Islam" title="Islam">Islam</a>, he said, "I like Islam: it is a consistent [or consequential] idea of religion and open-minded."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44"><a href="#cite_note-44">[44]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Legacy">Legacy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Kurt_G%C3%B6del&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Legacy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Kurt_G%C3%B6del_Society" title="Kurt Gödel Society">Kurt Gödel Society</a>, founded in 1987, was named in his honor. It is an international organization for the promotion of research in logic, philosophy, and the <a href="/wiki/History_of_mathematics" title="History of mathematics">history of mathematics</a>. The <a href="/wiki/University_of_Vienna" title="University of Vienna">University of Vienna</a> hosts the Kurt Gödel Research Center for Mathematical Logic. The <a href="/wiki/Association_for_Symbolic_Logic" title="Association for Symbolic Logic">Association for Symbolic Logic</a> has invited an annual Kurt Gödel lecturer each year since 1990. <a class="external text" href="http://www.bbaw.de/en/research/goedel" rel="nofollow">Gödel's Philosophical Notebooks</a> are edited at the <a class="external text" href="http://www.bbaw.de/en/research/goedel" rel="nofollow">Kurt Gödel Research Centre</a> which is situated at the <a class="external text" href="http://www.bbaw.de/en/academy" rel="nofollow">Berlin-Brandenburg Academy of Sciences and Humanities</a> in Germany.
</p><p>Five volumes of Gödel's collected works have been published. The first two include his publications; the third includes unpublished manuscripts from his <span title="German-language text"><i lang="de">Nachlass</i></span>, and the final two include correspondence.
</p><p>In 2005 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/John_W._Dawson,_Jr" title="John W. Dawson, Jr">John Dawson</a> published a biography of Gödel, <i>Logical Dilemmas: The Life and Work of Kurt Gödel</i> (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/A._K._Peters" title="A. K. Peters">A. K. Peters</a>, Wellesley, MA, <style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1067248974">.mw-parser-output cite.citation{font-style:inherit;word-wrap:break-word}.mw-parser-output .citation q{quotes:"\"""\"""'""'"}.mw-parser-output .citation:target{background-color:rgba(0,127,255,0.133)}.mw-parser-output .id-lock-free a,.mw-parser-output .citation .cs1-lock-free a{background:linear-gradient(transparent,transparent),url("//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/6/65/Lock-green.svg")right 0.1em center/9px no-repeat}.mw-parser-output .id-lock-limited a,.mw-parser-output .citation .cs1-lock-subscription a{background:linear-gradient(transparent,transparent),url("//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/a/aa/Lock-red-alt-2.svg")right 0.1em center/9px href="/wiki/ISBN_(identifier)" title="ISBN (identifier)">ISBN</a> <a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/1-56881-256-6" title="Special:BookSources/1-56881-256-6">1-56881-256-6</a>). <a href="/wiki/Stephen_Budiansky" title="Stephen Budiansky">Stephen Budiansky</a>'s book about Gödel's life, <i>Journey to the Edge of Reason: The Life of Kurt Gödel</i> (<a href="/wiki/W._W._Norton_%26_Company" title="W. W. Norton &amp; Company">W. W. Norton &amp; Company</a>, New York City, NY, <link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/ISBN_(identifier)" title="ISBN (identifier)">ISBN</a> <a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/978-0-393-35820-9" title="Special:BookSources/978-0-393-35820-9">978-0-393-35820-9</a>), was a <a href="/wiki/The_New_York_Times" title="The New York Times"><i>New York Times</i></a> Critics' Top Book of 2021.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45"><a href="#cite_note-45">[45]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Douglas_Hofstadter" title="Douglas Hofstadter">Douglas Hofstadter</a> wrote the 1979 book <span title="German-language text"><i lang="de"><a href="/wiki/G%C3%B6del,_Escher,_Bach" title="Gödel, Escher, Bach">Gödel, Escher, Bach</a></i></span> to celebrate the work and ideas of Gödel, <a href="/wiki/M._C._Escher" title="M. C. Escher">M. C. Escher</a> and <a href="/wiki/Johann_Sebastian_Bach" title="Johann Sebastian Bach">Johann Sebastian Bach</a>. It partly explores the ramifications of the fact that Gödel's incompleteness theorem can be applied to any <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Turing-complete" title="Turing-complete">Turing-complete</a> computational system, which may include the <a href="/wiki/Human_brain" title="Human brain">human brain</a>.
</p><p>Gödel was also one of four mathematicians examined in <a href="/wiki/David_Malone_(independent_filmmaker)" title="David Malone (independent filmmaker)">David Malone</a>'s 2008 <a href="/wiki/BBC" title="BBC">BBC</a> documentary <i>Dangerous Knowledge</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46"><a href="#cite_note-46">[46]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Lou_Jacobi" title="Lou Jacobi">Lou Jacobi</a> plays Gödel in the 1994 film <i><a href="/wiki/I.Q._(film)" title="I.Q. (film)">I.Q.</a></i>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Bibliography">Bibliography</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Kurt_G%C3%B6del&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Bibliography">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Important_publications">Important publications</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Kurt_G%C3%B6del&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Important publications">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In German:
</p>
<ul><li>1930, "Die Vollständigkeit der Axiome des logischen Funktionenkalküls." <i>Monatshefte für Mathematik und Physik</i> <b>37</b>: 349–60.</li>
<li>1931, "Über formal unentscheidbare Sätze der <i><a href="/wiki/Principia_Mathematica" title="Principia Mathematica">Principia Mathematica</a></i> und verwandter Systeme, I." <i>Monatshefte für Mathematik und Physik</i> <b>38</b>: 173–98.</li>
<li>1932, "Zum intuitionistischen Aussagenkalkül", <i>Anzeiger Akademie der Wissenschaften Wien</i> <b>69</b>: 65–66.</li></ul>
<p>In English:
</p>
<ul><li>1940. <i>The Consistency of the Axiom of Choice and of the Generalized Continuum Hypothesis with the Axioms of Set Theory.</i> Princeton University Press.</li>
<li>1947. "What is Cantor's continuum problem?" <i>The American Mathematical Monthly 54</i>: 515–25. Revised version in <a href="/wiki/Paul_Benacerraf" title="Paul Benacerraf">Paul Benacerraf</a> and <a href="/wiki/Hilary_Putnam" title="Hilary Putnam">Hilary Putnam</a>, eds., 1984 (1964). <i>Philosophy of Mathematics: Selected Readings</i>. Cambridge Univ. Press: 470–85.</li>
<li>1950, "Rotating Universes in General Relativity Theory." <i>Proceedings of the international Congress of Mathematicians in Cambridge,</i> Vol. 1, pp. 175–81.</li></ul>
<p>In English translation:
</p>

Meltzer, with a comprehensive introduction by <a href="/wiki/R._B._Braithwaite" title="R. B. Braithwaite">Richard Braithwaite</a>. Dover reprint of the 1962 Basic Books edition.</li>

From p. 80, which quotes Rudolf Gödel, Kurt's brother and a medical doctor. The words "a severe nervous crisis", and the judgement that the Schlick assassination was its trigger, are from the Rudolf Gödel quote. Rudolf knew Kurt well in those years.</span>

<li id="cite_note-25"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-25">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Dawson 1997, pp. 179–80. The story of Gödel's citizenship hearing is repeated in many versions. Dawson's account is the most carefully researched, but was written before the rediscovery of Morgenstern's written account. Most other accounts appear to be based on Dawson, hearsay or speculation.</span>
</li>

<li id="cite_note-38"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-38">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><cite class="citation book cs1" id="CITEREFTucker_McElroy2005">Tucker McElroy (2005). <a class="external text" href="https://archive.org/details/tozofmathematici0000mcel/page/118" rel="nofollow"><i>A to Z of Mathematicians</i></a>. Infobase Publishing. p. <a class="external text" href="https://archive.org/details/tozofmathematici0000mcel/page/118" rel="nofollow">118</a>. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/ISBN_(identifier)" title="ISBN (identifier)">ISBN</a> <a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/978-0-8160-5338-4" title="Special:BookSources/978-0-8160-5338-4"><bdi>978-0-8160-5338-4</bdi></a>. <q>Gödel had a happy childhood, and was called "Mr. Why" by his family, due to his numerous questions. He was baptized as a Lutheran, and re-mained a theist (a believer in a personal God) throughout his life.</q></cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft.btitle=A+to+Z+of+Mathematicians&amp;rft.pages=118&amp;rft.pub=Infobase+Publishing&amp;rft.date=2005&amp;rft.isbn=978-0-8160-5338-4&amp;rft.au=Tucker+McElroy&amp;rft_id=https%3A%2F%2Farchive.org%2Fdetails%2Ftozofmathematici0000mcel%2Fpage%2F118&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AKurt+G%C3%B6del"></span></span>

<li id="cite_note-FOOTNOTEWang1996[httpsbooksgooglecomaubooksidpckvCy6L_ocCpgPA8_8]-39"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWang1996[httpsbooksgooglecomaubooksidpckvCy6L_ocCpgPA8_8]_39-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="#CITEREFWang1996">Wang 1996</a>, p. <a class="external text" href="https://books.google.com.au/books?id=pckvCy6L_ocC&amp;pg=PA8" rel="nofollow">8</a>.</span>

<li id="cite_note-FOOTNOTEWang1996104-105-40"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWang1996104-105_40-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="#CITEREFWang1996">Wang 1996</a>, p. 104-105.</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-41"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-41">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Gödel's answer to a special questionnaire sent him by the sociologist Burke Grandjean. This answer is quoted directly in <a href="#CITEREFWang1987">Wang 1987</a>, p. <a class="external text" href="https://books.google.com.au/books?id=wLLePwhDOMYC&amp;pg=PA18" rel="nofollow">18</a>, and indirectly in <a href="#CITEREFWang1996">Wang 1996</a>, p. 112. It's also quoted directly in <a href="#CITEREFDawson1997">Dawson 1997</a>, p. 6, who cites <a href="#CITEREFWang1987">Wang 1987</a>. The Grandjean questionnaire is perhaps the most extended autobiographical item in Gödel's papers. Gödel filled it out in pencil and wrote a cover letter, but he never returned it. "Theistic" is italicized in both <a href="#CITEREFWang1987">Wang 1987</a> and <a href="#CITEREFWang1996">Wang 1996</a>. It is possible that this italicization is Wang's and not Gödel's. The quote follows <a href="#CITEREFWang1987">Wang 1987</a>, with two corrections taken from <a href="#CITEREFWang1996">Wang 1996</a>. <a href="#CITEREFWang1987">Wang 1987</a> reads "Baptist Lutheran" where <a href="#CITEREFWang1996">Wang 1996</a> has "baptized Lutheran". <a href="#CITEREFWang1987">Wang 1987</a> has "rel. cong.", which in <a href="#CITEREFWang1996">Wang 1996</a> is expanded to "religious congregation".</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-FOOTNOTEWang1996316-42"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWang1996316_42-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="#CITEREFWang1996">Wang 1996</a>, p. 316.</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-FOOTNOTEWang199651-43"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWang199651_43-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="#CITEREFWang1996">Wang 1996</a>, p. 51.</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-44"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-44">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="#CITEREFWang1996">Wang 1996</a>, p. 148, 4.4.3. It is one of Gödel's observations, made between 16 November and 7 December 1975, which Wang found hard to classify under the main topics considered elsewhere in the book.</span>
</li>
